# Anchor Interview

## Summary

This session began with a comprehensive interview to improve the implementation plan, leading to a major renderer decision, followed by consistency fixes and an operator redesign.

**Implementation Plan Interview:**
- Conducted detailed interview using AskUserQuestion tool covering all engine requirements
- Software renderer motivations discussed: aesthetic, simplicity, web reliability
- Key discoveries that changed the calculus:
  - Discrete rotation was only "acceptable tradeoff" — not a desired aesthetic
  - Additive blending is essential (explosions, energy effects)
  - Console targets (Switch, Xbox, PlayStation) require GPU APIs — incompatible with software rendering
  - You already know GLSL and have written 500+ line shaders; the concern was preference, not capability
  - Pixel control examples were mostly theoretical (Noita-style) rather than needed for ball combat games
- Audio gap identified: SDL_mixer insufficient (no pitch shifting), needs research (miniaudio vs SoLoud)
- Physics confirmed: Box2D 3.x, fixed 60fps timestep for determinism
- Platform targets: Windows, Linux, Web primary; mobile/console if games succeed
- Debug workflow: print debugging + error screen sufficient
- C structure: single `anchor.c`, resources live forever, single executable distribution
- Distant artistic vision revealed: "7 stories that are 7 games" with merged game/narrative elements
- **Final decision: OpenGL** — clean decision with no "software-like mental model" compromise

**Documentation Updates:**
- Created new `ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` with OpenGL, 10 phases, TBD audio
- Deleted old `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` (software renderer based)
- Updated ANCHOR.md: removed software renderer sections (~200 lines), added OpenGL rendering
- Updated CLAUDE.md: changed references from software rendering to OpenGL

**Consistency Fixes (multiple rounds, including pre-compaction work):**
- Timer architecture: child object with `after`, `every`, `tween` methods via `self.timer:after(...)`
- Engine object pattern: timer/spring/collider are tree objects named after themselves by default
- Spring examples: single springs use `spring(1, 200, 10)` → `self.spring`; multiples use explicit names
- Collider naming: optional name parameter separate from physics tag
- Color format: `an.colors.white` without `[0]` indexing
- Platforms: Linux as primary alongside Windows and Web
- Changed `add_child` → `add` in reference implementation
- Build function example: `self:add(timer())` → `self + timer()`

**High-Level Engine Analysis:**
- Confirmed locality principle genuinely achieved through operator pipeline
- Confirmed no-bureaucracy goal achieved via globals and automatic cleanup
- Tree-based ownership identified as core elegant insight
- Collision handling and draw order noted as necessary tradeoffs that break locality
- Critiqued `%` and `//` operators as arbitrary with no mnemonic — led to redesign

**Operator Redesign:**
- Replaced arbitrary `%` (early) and `//` (late) with unified `/` operator using phase helpers
- Explored multiple alias combinations: A/E (A conflicted with `all()`), Y/E, T/E, X/N
- **Final aliases:** E (object/Entity), U (early/Under), L (late/Late), X (named/eXplicit), A (all/All)
- All runic (angular, symmetrical letters), all with decent mnemonics
- `/` operator detects `U`, `L`, and `X` helpers via `__early`, `__late`, or string key patterns
- Helper signatures support both forms: `U(fn)` and `U('name', fn)`
- Updated all three documentation files with new operator syntax

**Final Cleanup:**
- Removed all mentions of deprecated `%` and `//` operators
- Fixed simplified alias definitions to show full signatures

**Files modified:**
- Created: `docs/ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md`
- Deleted: `docs/IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md`
- Updated: `docs/ANCHOR.md`, `.claude/CLAUDE.md`
- Created: `yue/examples.yue` (renamed from `operator_comparison.yue`)

---

Yes, I'm familiar with the AskUserQuestion tool. It's one of my available tools that allows me to ask you questions during our conversation when I need clarification, want to validate assumptions, or need help making decisions.

**How it works:**

- I can ask 1-4 questions at once
- Each question has a short header (max 12 chars) displayed as a chip/tag
- I provide 2-4 options per question, each with a label and description
- You can always select "Other" to provide custom text input
- There's a `multiSelect` option for questions where multiple answers apply

**When I'd use it:**

- Gathering your preferences or requirements
- Clarifying ambiguous instructions
- Getting decisions on implementation choices as I work
- Offering choices about which direction to take

**Example scenario:**

If you asked me to "add authentication to the app," I might use AskUserQuestion to ask:

```
Question: "Which authentication method should we use?"
Header: "Auth method"
Options:
  - JWT tokens (stateless, good for APIs)
  - Session cookies (traditional, simpler for web apps)
  - OAuth2 (delegate to external providers)
```

It's particularly useful for the kinds of architectural and implementation decisions that come up when working on Anchor or games built with it — where you have specific opinions and I shouldn't just guess.

Is there something specific you'd like me to ask you about, or were you curious about how it functions?

**Single-select (`multiSelect: false`)**: You can only pick ONE answer. Pressing enter submits that one choice. You can also select "Other" to type a custom response.

**Multi-select (`multiSelect: true`)**: You can select MULTIPLE answers before submitting. This is better for questions where several options might apply simultaneously.

1. **Discrete rotation is a tradeoff, not a goal** — this actually weakens the software renderer case since OpenGL could give smooth rotation with the same chunky pixel aesthetic (nearest-neighbor filtering at 480×270).

2. **Additive blending is essential** — doable in software but trivial in OpenGL (`glBlendFunc`). This is a point in OpenGL's favor.

3. **Minimal animation** — good, no complex animation system needed.

4. **Varies wildly on physics bodies** — this makes performance harder to predict.

This round revealed something critical: **Consoles are a target.** 

This is a major factor for the renderer decision. Console platforms (Switch, Xbox, PlayStation) essentially require GPU-based rendering — you don't get direct framebuffer access like software rendering assumes. Software rendering would be a dead end for console ports.

Combined with:
- Discrete rotations being unwanted (OpenGL gives smooth)
- Additive blending being essential (trivial in OpenGL)
- "All of the above" chaos (performance matters)
- Shader complexity being a concern (solvable with a thin abstraction)

The case for OpenGL is strengthening significantly. Let me explore this more, plus other areas:

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

Now we're getting to the heart of it. The core tension isn't really about complexity or reliability — it's about **creative freedom**:

> "Controlling pixels individually with the software renderer is much more freeing than being bound by shaders... having complete control over individual pixels feels much more intuitive than writing effects using shaders."

This is a legitimate creative preference. The question becomes: what specific effects need that pixel-level control, and is there a way to get that freedom while keeping console viability?

Let's have that discussion about the renderer. I'll lay out my analysis based on everything you've shared.

---

## The Renderer Decision: Software vs. OpenGL

### What You Want (distilled from our conversation)

1. **Pixel-level control** feels more intuitive than shaders for effects
2. **Additive blending** is essential (explosions, energy effects)
3. **Smooth rotation** would be preferred over discrete angles (if easy)
4. **Chunky pixel aesthetic** at 480×270 or similar
5. **Simplicity** — don't want to fight GPU state management
6. **Web deployment** works reliably
7. **Console-compatible** if a game succeeds (distant concern)
8. **Custom shaders** available if you go OpenGL route (you know GLSL, just don't love it)

### Software Renderer: Honest Assessment

**Pros:**
- Direct pixel manipulation — iterate pixels, apply any math
- No GPU state to manage, no driver bugs
- Intuitive for effects like pixel sorting
- Single code path for all platforms
- Debugging is just "look at the pixel array"

**Cons:**
- **Performance is unverified** at your scale (200+ sprites, alpha blending, additive blending)
- **Additive blending** requires a separate blend loop per pixel (doable but adds cost)
- **Smooth rotation** requires per-pixel sampling (expensive) or precomputed frames (memory)
- **Console-incompatible** — would need complete rewrite for GPU
- **Scaling limits** — if a game idea needs 500 particles, you might hit a wall

**The core risk:** You're betting that 480×270 is small enough that brute-force software rendering stays fast. This might be true! But you haven't tested it, and "I'll switch to GPU if it's slow" is a significant pivot mid-development.

### OpenGL: Honest Assessment

**Pros:**
- **Smooth rotation** is free (just change the angle uniform)
- **Additive blending** is one line: `glBlendFunc(GL_SRC_ALPHA, GL_ONE)`
- **Performance headroom** — GPU handles thousands of sprites trivially
- **Console-portable** — same mental model, different backend API
- **Custom shaders** give pixel-level control *when you need it*
- You already know GLSL (500+ line shaders)

**Cons:**
- **State management complexity** — glEnable/glDisable, binding order
- **Shader debugging** is painful compared to stepping through pixel loops
- **You don't enjoy it** — this is real and matters for motivation
- **WebGL quirks** — though WebGL 1.0 is quite stable now

**The key insight:** With a thin abstraction layer, you'd write `layer:circle()` not `glDrawArrays()`. The shader complexity is hidden until you need custom effects. And when you *do* need custom effects, shaders give you per-pixel control — just expressed differently.

### My Recommendation

**Start with OpenGL, but with a software-renderer-like mental model.**

Here's why:

1. **You can get pixel control via shaders.** Pixel sorting? Write a pixel sort shader. Per-pixel color manipulation? Fragment shader. The operations are the same, just expressed in GLSL instead of a loop. With my help, we can write any pixel effect you can describe.

2. **Your real concern is intuition, not capability.** You *know* GLSL. You just find it unintuitive. But with me as a collaborator, you can describe the effect you want, and I translate it to GLSL. Your intuition drives, I handle the syntax.

3. **The backup plan is risky.** "Switch to GPU if slow" sounds easy but it's a significant rewrite. Starting with GPU means the backup is "optimize the shaders" — a much smaller change.

4. **Additive blending + smooth rotation + performance** all come free with OpenGL. These aren't minor conveniences — they're things you said you want.

5. **The chunky pixel aesthetic is preserved.** Render to a 480×270 framebuffer with nearest-neighbor filtering. The pixels stay chunky. This isn't a tradeoff.

### The Counter-Argument (Why Software Might Still Be Right)

If your games stay at SNKRX scale (50-100 objects, simple effects), software rendering at 480×270 is probably fine. The pixel control intuition matters for creative flow. And you've shipped games before — you know what you actually use.

The honest truth: **SNKRX and BYTEPATH don't use effects that require pixel-level control.** Circles, shapes, screen shake, particles — all GPU-friendly. The pixel control argument is about *potential* effects you *might* want, not effects you've actually needed.

---

**My question for you:** Is the pixel control preference based on effects you've actually wanted but couldn't achieve in LÖVE? Or is it more about creative possibility space — wanting the freedom even if you haven't used it?

This might determine the answer.

> OK, let's just go with OpenGL then, and not even keep a "software-renderer-like" mental model.

Good. Clean decision. OpenGL it is.

**Major changes from the original plan:**
- **OpenGL renderer** instead of software rendering
- **Smooth rotation** is now free (shader-based)
- **Additive blending** is a one-line API call
- **Custom shaders** are available from the start
- **Audio library is TBD** — flagged for research (miniaudio vs SoLoud) since SDL_mixer doesn't support pitch shifting
- **Web build is Phase 2** (right after skeleton) to catch platform issues early
- **Effects phase is separate** from rendering, focused on post-processing shaders
- **Cleaner phase organization** — 10 phases total, each with clear deliverable

**Key decisions captured:**
- Single `anchor.c` file
- Resources live forever (no unloading)
- Fixed timestep for determinism
- Per-game configurable resolution
- Single executable distribution with embedded assets

**Deferred items explicitly listed:**
- Gamepad, Steam Input
- Hot reload, debug tools
- Save/load, networking
- Full UI system

  1365→
  1366→## Rendering Architecture
  1367→
  1368→### Software Renderer
  1369→
  1370→Anchor uses a CPU-based software renderer targeting 480×270 or 640×360 resolution (depending on game). This approach provides direct pixel manipulation, simpler debugging, and consistent behavior across platforms.
  1371→
  1372→### Layers = Framebuffers
  1373→
  1374→Each layer is a framebuffer. Draw commands are batched per layer and flushed at end of frame:
  1375→
  1376→1. Lua calls `layer:circle()`, `layer:draw_image()`, etc.
  1377→2. Each call appends to that layer's command buffer
  1378→3. At end of frame, C processes all commands in a tight loop
  1379→4. Effects are applied per-layer (C functions, configured from Lua)
  1380→5. Layers composite to screen via alpha blending
  1381→
  1382→### Sprites
  1383→
  1384→Sprites use precomputed rotations (16 or 32 angles) with runtime scaling. This trades memory for computation and creates a distinctive aesthetic with discrete rotation angles.
  1385→
  1386→### Effects
  1387→
  1388→Post-processing effects are implemented in C and configured from Lua:
  1389→
... [180 more lines]</code></pre>

  1366→## Rendering Architecture
  1367→
  1368→### OpenGL Renderer
  1369→
  1370→Anchor uses OpenGL 3.3 Core Profile for rendering, targeting 480×270 or 640×360 base resolution (per-game configurable). The low resolution is rendered to a framebuffer texture, then integer-scaled to the window with nearest-neighbor filtering for crisp pixels.
  1371→
  1372→### Layers
  1373→
  1374→Each layer is a framebuffer texture. Draw commands are batched per layer:
  1375→
  1376→1. Lua calls `layer:circle()`, `layer:draw_image()`, etc.
  1377→2. Geometry is batched into vertex buffers
  1378→3. At end of frame, layers are rendered via draw calls
  1379→4. Post-processing shaders are applied per-layer
  1380→5. Layers composite to screen in defined order
  1381→
  1382→### Sprites
  1383→
  1384→Sprites are rendered as textured quads with smooth rotation (handled by the GPU). No precomputed rotation frames needed.
  1385→
  1386→### Blend Modes
  1387→
  1388→Two blend modes available per draw call:
  1389→- **Alpha blending** (default): standard transparency
  1390→- **Additive blending**: for glows, explosions, energy effects
... [35 more lines]</code></pre>

  1425→## OpenGL Implementation Notes
  1426→
  1427→This section provides guidance for the OpenGL renderer implementation.
  1428→
  1429→### Core Concepts
  1430→
  1431→**Framebuffer Objects (FBOs):** Each layer renders to an FBO at the game's base resolution (e.g., 480×270). This allows post-processing and composition.
  1432→
  1433→**Batch Rendering:** Geometry is accumulated into vertex buffers and drawn in batches to minimize draw calls. Batch breaks occur on texture/shader/blend mode changes.
  1434→
  1435→**Shaders:** A small set of shaders handles all rendering:
  1436→- Sprite shader (textured quads with transform)
  1437→- Shape shader (circles, rectangles, lines)
  1438→- Post-process shaders (outline, tint, brightness, custom)
  1439→
  1440→### Frame Flow
  1441→
  1442→```
  1443→1. For each layer:
  1444→   - Bind layer's FBO
  1445→   - Clear to transparent
  1446→   - Batch and draw all geometry
  1447→   - Apply post-process shader if configured
  1448→
  1449→2. Bind screen framebuffer
... [38 more lines]</code></pre>

  1282→for _, event in ipairs(an:physics_get_contact_enter('ball', 'wall')) do

I've read all three files. Here's my analysis of issues found:

## Issues Found

### 1. Contradictions Between Files

**1.1 Pitch shifting in Deferred Features (ANCHOR.md:1468)**
```
- Advanced audio (pitch shifting, positional)
```
But the implementation plan says pitch shifting is **essential** and needed immediately. This contradicts our interview where you said pitch shifting is needed right away.

**1.2 File Structure contradiction (ANCHOR.md:1409-1415)**
Shows multiple C files (`main.c`, `an_render.c`, `an_physics.c`, etc.) but the implementation plan explicitly says "Single anchor.c" and the key decisions table confirms this.

**1.3 CLAUDE.md references removed section**
Line 10 says to read ANCHOR.md for "The mental model (engine services vs object tree)" but we just removed the Engine Services section.

**1.4 Sound API inconsistency**
CLAUDE.md line 247: `an:sound_play(bounce_sound, speed / 100)` (number as second arg)
Implementation plan: `an:sound_play(hit, {volume = 0.5, pitch = 1.2})` (table with named params)

**1.5 Rectangle API inconsistency**
ANCHOR.md: `game:rectangle(x, y, w, h, rx, ry, color)` (with rounded corners?)
Implementation plan: `game:rectangle(x, y, w, h, color)` (no rounded corners)

### 2. Redundancy

**2.1 Rendering Architecture + OpenGL Implementation Notes overlap**
Both sections describe layers as framebuffers, batch rendering, frame flow, blend modes. Should consolidate.

**2.2 Migration Path duplicates Phase Summary**
ANCHOR.md has a 12-step migration path that's essentially the same as the implementation plan's phases.

**2.3 Build System and Resolution sections are too sparse**
One or two lines each, adding no value beyond the implementation plan.

### 3. Unclear/Awkward Design

**3.1 `mouse_world_position(camera)` in implementation plan**
Suggests C knows about cameras, but camera is supposed to be pure YueScript. Should be `camera:screen_to_world(mx, my)` instead.

**3.2 `an.colors.white[0]` in examples**
Used in several places but never documented. Is there a built-in color palette?

**3.3 Timer initialization confusion**
ANCHOR.md shows `self:timer()` as a method to "enable timer functionality", but the object class code shows timers as internal arrays. Is there a timer child object or just an array?

**3.4 YueScript directory structure**
Implementation plan shows `timer.yue`, `spring.yue`, `collider.yue` as separate files, but ANCHOR.md shows collider as a simple factory function. Are these separate files or part of object.yue?

>    - Discrete rotation (acceptable tradeoff, not desired)

> Summary:
> 1. Primary Request and Intent:
>    The user asked me to dramatically improve the IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN via an in-depth interview using AskUserQuestion tool. The interview covered technical implementation, features, concerns, and tradeoffs. A major tension was the software renderer vs OpenGL decision. After extensive discussion, the user decided on OpenGL. I then rewrote the plan as ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md, deleted the old plan, and updated all related documentation. The final request was to do a comprehensive consistency check across ANCHOR.md, ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md, and CLAUDE.md for inconsistencies, redundancies, misplaced sections, and logic issues.
> 
> 2. Key Technical Concepts:
>    - OpenGL 3.3 Core Profile (chosen over software renderer)
>    - WebGL 2.0 compatibility for web builds
>    - Box2D 3.1 physics with sensor/contact events
>    - YueScript compiled to Lua at build-time with `-r` flag
>    - Fixed timestep (60fps) for determinism/replay support
>    - Single anchor.c monolithic C file
>    - Pitch shifting essential for audio (SDL_mixer insufficient, need miniaudio or SoLoud)
>    - Layers as framebuffer objects
>    - Object tree with operators (^, %, /, //, +, >>)
>    - Resources live forever (no unloading)
>    - Single executable distribution with embedded assets
>    - Platform targets: Windows (primary), Web, Linux, Mobile, Consoles (eventually)
> 
> 3. Files and Code Sections:
>    - `/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md`

     2→
     3→C engine with YueScript scripting, OpenGL rendering, targeting Windows and Web.

     6→
     7→## Summary of Key Decisions
     8→
     9→| Area | Decision | Rationale |
    10→|------|----------|-----------|
    11→| Renderer | OpenGL | Smooth rotation, additive blending, performance headroom, console-portable |

    15→| Timestep | Fixed 60fps | Determinism matters for potential replay support |
    16→| Resolution | Per-game configurable | 480×270, 640×360, or custom; integer scaling |
    17→| C Structure | Single anchor.c | Monolithic file, easier navigation |
    18→| Resources | Live forever | Games are small enough; no unloading needed |

<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
  1250→- **Platforms:** Windows (primary), Web (Emscripten), Linux (eventually)
  1251→
  1252→---
  1253→
  1254→## Rendering
  1255→
  1256→Anchor uses OpenGL 3.3 Core Profile (WebGL 2.0 compatible) for rendering, targeting 480×270 or 640×360 base resolution (per-game configurable). The low resolution is rendered to a framebuffer texture, then integer-scaled to the window with nearest-neighbor filtering for crisp pixels.
  1257→
  1258→### Layers
  1259→
  1260→Each layer is a framebuffer object (FBO). Draw commands are batched per layer:
  1261→
  1262→1. Lua calls `layer:circle()`, `layer:draw_image()`, etc.
  1263→2. Geometry is batched into vertex buffers (batch breaks on texture/shader/blend mode changes)
  1264→3. At end of frame, layers are rendered via draw calls
  1265→4. Post-processing shaders are applied per-layer
  1266→5. Layers composite to screen in defined order
  1267→
  1268→```lua
  1269→game = an:layer('game')
  1270→effects = an:layer('effects')
  1271→ui = an:layer('ui')
  1272→
  1273→game:circle(x, y, radius, color)
  1274→game:rectangle(x, y, w, h, color)
  1275→game:line(x1, y1, x2, y2, color, width)
  1276→game:draw_image(img, x, y, r, sx, sy, ox, oy, color)
  1277→```
  1278→
... [51 more lines]</code></pre>

</details>

<pre><code>1325→
  1326→## Error Handling
  1327→
  1328→LÖVE-style protected calls. Errors display stacktrace on screen; application continues running in error state.
  1329→
  1330→```c
  1331→if (lua_pcall(L, nargs, nresults, error_handler) != LUA_OK) {
  1332→    strncpy(error_message, lua_tostring(L, -1), sizeof(error_message));
  1333→    app_state = STATE_ERROR;
  1334→}
  1335→```
  1336→
  1337→No restart on keypress — user closes and reopens the application.
  1338→
  1339→---
  1340→
  1341→## Resolution and Scaling
  1342→
  1343→Fixed resolution per game (480×270 or 640×360), integer scaled with letterboxing. Nearest-neighbor filtering for crisp pixels.

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
  1312→```
  1313→
  1314→Built-in effects: `outline`, `tint`, `brightness`. Additional effects via custom shaders.
  1315→
  1316→### Colors
  1317→
  1318→Colors are 32-bit RGBA values (e.g., `0xFF0000FF` for red). A built-in palette is available:
  1319→
  1320→```lua
  1321→an.colors.white[0]   -- pure white
  1322→an.colors.black[0]   -- pure black
  1323→an.colors.red[0]     -- etc.
  1324→```
  1325→
  1326→Palette colors have brightness variants: `[0]` is the base color, negative indices are darker, positive are lighter.
  1327→
  1328→### Draw Order
  1329→
  1330→Draw order within a layer is submission order (when Lua calls draw functions), not tree order.
  1331→
  1332→### WebGL Compatibility
  1333→
  1334→OpenGL 3.3 Core Profile maps cleanly to WebGL 2.0. Avoid geometry shaders, compute shaders, and extension-dependent features.
  1335→
  1336→---</code></pre>

   298→```
   299→
   300→Each spring object represents one spring with its own value.
   301→
   302→### Timer
   303→
   304→A timer object provides delayed and repeating callbacks, plus tweening. Timer methods (`after`, `every`, `tween`) auto-initialize if needed, so explicit `self:timer()` is optional:
   305→
   306→```lua
   307→-- One-shot (anonymous)
   308→self:after(0.5, function() self.can_shoot = true end)
   309→
   310→-- One-shot (named — can be cancelled/replaced)
   311→self:after(0.15, 'flash', function() self.flashing = false end)

All consistency fixes are now complete. Here's a summary of what was fixed:

**ANCHOR.md:**
1. **File Structure** - Changed from multiple C files to single `anchor.c` monolithic structure
2. **Consolidated Rendering sections** - Merged "Rendering Architecture" and "OpenGL Implementation Notes" into a single "Rendering" section with all relevant subsections
3. **Removed Migration Path** - Deleted redundant section that duplicated the implementation plan
4. **Merged Build System + Resolution** - Combined sparse sections into "Build and Distribution" with Resolution, Build Scripts, and Distribution subsections
5. **Documented Colors** - Added Colors subsection explaining the `an.colors` palette system
6. **Clarified Timer initialization** - Updated Timer section to note that `after()`, `every()`, `tween()` auto-initialize, so explicit `self:timer()` is optional
7. **Updated Table of Contents** - Reflects all section changes

**CLAUDE.md:**
1. **Updated "Read First"** - Changed "engine services vs object tree" to "object tree model (tree-based ownership, automatic cleanup)" since Engine Services section was removed
2. **Fixed Sound API** - Changed `an:sound_play(bounce_sound, speed / 100)` to `an:sound_play(bounce_sound, {volume = speed / 100})` to match table format

## Questions for You

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

<pre><code>User has answered your questions: "Color indices use [0] as base (an.colors.white[0]), but Lua tables are 1-indexed. Is this intentional C-style indexing, or should it be [1] as base with negatives darker and positives lighter?"="This was how I did colors in Anchor so far, but I think it's easier to just have direct colors and leave darker/brighter values for later. So just assume a default palette exists (an.colors.white, etc), and that colors themselves passed to functions follow whatever convention this is (is it a Lua table? C struct pointer? I don't know, but it doesn't matter API-wise)", "ANCHOR.md Drawing example uses self.body:get_position() but Collider section creates self.collider. Which name should the collider use?"="All mixin-like objects, timers, springs, colliders, and more that I ended up not mentioning, are not objects that live as children of their parents. So if the collider object has a body attribute, and you want to access that from the player, and the collider on the player is on the .collider attribute, it'd be self.collider.body. However, the collider exposes :get_position such that the parent doesn't have to access the internal attributes, so it'd be, from the parent, self.collider:get_position(). The one change we made to the engine is that EVERY object is a part of the tree now, so that's how it goes.", "Objects can have special methods (update:, late_update:) AND actions (/ operator). If both exist, what happens?"="Both run", "Timers are described as 'child objects' but stored in @timers array, not @children. Which is the true model?"="Could you show me the example exactly? They're supposed to be child objects, I don't remember where a timer arrays exists.". You can now continue with the user's answers in mind.</code></pre>

</details>

Thanks for the clarifications! Let me show you the timer array issue and ask the remaining questions:

**Timer array location** - In ANCHOR.md lines 1036-1059, the YueScript class implementation shows:

```yuescript
after: (delay, name_or_fn, fn) =>
  @\timer! unless @timers
  if type(name_or_fn) == 'string'
    ...
    table.insert @timers, timer_obj
  else
    table.insert @timers, {delay: delay, elapsed: 0, fn: name_or_fn}
```

This uses `@timers` as an array, not `@children`. If timers should be child objects, this reference implementation needs to change.

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

<pre><code>User has answered your questions: "The YueScript reference implementation stores timers in @timers array. Should this be changed to add timers as children via @\add_child?"="That's perhaps unfortunate naming, but @timers inside the timer object is each after, every, tween, etc call that's running. Each high-level "timer" can have multiple internal "timers", each being after, every, etc, calls that were called for that high-level timer. Does this make sense? Perhaps we should call the internal ones another name. The internal ones should not be children because that's just how the timer object itself works, in the same way that the box2d body should be a child of the collider object, because the point of the collider is to hide the box2d complexity, not expose it to the tree.", "For physics_get_contact_enter('ball', 'wall'), does tag order matter? Is c.a always the first tag and c.b the second?"="Don't care yet", "Layer composition order - what determines which layer renders on top?"="Creation order by default, can be specified with named order list if needed.", "Build scripts: You're on Linux. Should build.sh handle both Linux and Web, or have separate scripts?"="build.sh for Linux". You can now continue with the user's answers in mind.</code></pre>

</details>

That makes sense! So there are two levels:
- **Timer object** (child of parent) - the high-level timer system
- **timer entries** (inside Timer's `@timers`) - individual `after`/`every`/`tween` calls

I'll suggest renaming the internal array to `@entries` or `@callbacks` to avoid confusion.

If an object has name='enemy' AND tag 'enemy', is it matched once or twice? And can multiple objects have the same name?"="Defer to implementation", "Should rectangle support rounded corners? game:rectangle(x, y, w, h, color, rx, ry)?"="Separate function". You can now continue with the user's answers in mind.</code></pre>

<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
  1179→```
  1180→
  1181→Forgetting `super` means no `@children`, `@tags`, etc.
  1182→
  1183→### Special Methods
  1184→
  1185→The engine automatically calls these methods on objects if they exist:
  1186→
  1187→- `early_update(dt)` — called during early phase
  1188→- `update(dt)` — called during main phase
  1189→- `late_update(dt)` — called during late phase (often used for drawing)
  1190→- `destroy()` — called when object is removed from tree
  1191→
  1192→Define them as regular class methods:
  1193→```yuescript
  1194→class enemy extends object
  1195→  update: (dt) =&gt;
  1196→    @x, @y = @collider\get_position!
  1197→
  1198→  late_update: (dt) =&gt;
  1199→    game\circle @x, @y, @radius, @color
  1200→```
  1201→
  1202→**Methods and actions both run.** If an object has both an `update:` method and `/` actions, the method runs first, then the actions. This allows class-defined behavior to coexist with dynamically added behavior.
  1203→
  1204→### Colliders and Springs Are Factory Functions
  1205→
  1206→Colliders and springs are created by factory functions that return objects with specialized functionality:</code></pre>

</details>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
  1032→    else
  1033→      table.insert @late_actions, {fn: name_or_fn}
  1034→    @
  1035→  
  1036→  -- Timers (internal @callbacks array stores scheduled after/every/tween entries)
  1037→  after: (delay, name_or_fn, fn) =&gt;
  1038→    @\timer! unless @callbacks
  1039→    if type(name_or_fn) == 'string'
  1040→      name, func = name_or_fn, fn
  1041→      @[name]\kill! if @[name] and @[name].kill

  1043→      @[name] = timer_obj
  1044→      table.insert @callbacks, timer_obj
  1045→    else

  1047→    @

  1052→      name, func = name_or_fn, fn
  1053→      @[name]\kill! if @[name] and @[name].kill
  1054→      timer_obj = {name: name, interval: interval, elapsed: 0, fn: func, repeating: true, kill: -&gt; @dead = true}
  1055→      @[name] = timer_obj
  1056→      table.insert @callbacks, timer_obj
  1057→    else
  1058→      table.insert @callbacks, {interval: interval, elapsed: 0, fn: name_or_fn, repeating: true}
  1059→    @
  1060→  
... [3 more lines]</code></pre>

</details>

<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
  1252→### Layers
  1253→
  1254→Each layer is a framebuffer object (FBO). Draw commands are batched per layer:
  1255→
  1256→1. Lua calls `layer:circle()`, `layer:draw_image()`, etc.
  1257→2. Geometry is batched into vertex buffers (batch breaks on texture/shader/blend mode changes)
  1258→3. At end of frame, layers are rendered via draw calls
  1259→4. Post-processing shaders are applied per-layer
  1260→5. Layers composite to screen in creation order (first created = bottom, last = top)
  1261→
  1262→Layer order can be overridden if needed:
  1263→```lua
  1264→an:set_layer_order({'game', 'effects', 'ui'})
  1265→```
  1266→
  1267→```lua
  1268→game = an:layer('game')
  1269→effects = an:layer('effects')</code></pre>

</details>

All consistency fixes are complete. Here's a summary:

---

## Fixes Applied

**Platform & Build:**
1. **Linux is now a primary platform** (was "eventually") in ANCHOR.md and IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md
2. **Build scripts clarified:** `build.sh` for Linux, `build-web.sh` for Web (Emscripten)
3. **File structure updated** in both docs to reflect the three build scripts

**Colors:**
4. **Removed `[0]` indexing** from all color examples - now just `an.colors.white`
5. **Simplified Colors section** - removed brightness variant documentation

**API Consistency:**
6. **Fixed Drawing example** - `self.body:get_position()` → `self.collider:get_position()`
7. **Added `rounded_rectangle()`** as a separate function in both docs
8. **Added layer order documentation** - creation order by default, with `an:set_layer_order()` override

**Behavior Documentation:**
9. **Documented methods + actions both run** - methods first, then actions
10. **Renamed `@timers` to `@callbacks`** in Timer implementation to avoid confusion with the Timer object itself

---

## Decisions Deferred to Implementation

- Physics collision order (`c.a` vs `c.b` ordering)
- `all()` behavior (name vs tag matching, deduplication)

Important clarification! Colliders, springs, and timers should all be tree objects (classes extending object), not factory functions. Let me fix the documentation:

<details>
<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
  1200→```
  1201→
  1202→**Methods and actions both run.** If an object has both an `update:` method and `/` actions, the method runs first, then the actions. This allows class-defined behavior to coexist with dynamically added behavior.
  1203→
  1204→### Colliders, Springs, and Timers Are Tree Objects
  1205→
  1206→Colliders, springs, and timers are all proper tree objects (classes extending `object`). They live in `@children` like any other child, and die when their parent dies.
  1207→
  1208→```yuescript
  1209→class collider extends object
  1210→  new: (tag, body_type, shape_type, ...) =&gt;
  1211→    super 'collider'
  1212→    @body_id = an\physics_create_body tag, body_type, shape_type, ...
  1213→
  1214→  get_position: =&gt; an\physics_get_position @body_id
  1215→  set_velocity: (vx, vy) =&gt; an\physics_set_velocity @body_id, vx, vy
  1216→
  1217→  destroy: =&gt;
  1218→    an\physics_destroy_body @body_id
  1219→
  1220→-- Usage: added as child via + operator
  1221→@ + collider 'player', 'dynamic', 'circle', 12
  1222→```
  1223→
  1224→The `destroy` method is called automatically when the collider dies (when parent dies or when explicitly killed).
  1225→
  1226→### Method vs Property Access
  1227→
  1228→In YueScript:</code></pre>

</details>

<details>
<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   206→## Lifecycle
   207→
   208→### Update Order
   209→
   210→Each frame:
   211→
   212→1. **Collect all objects** — Traverse tree, gather all non-dead objects
   213→2. **Early actions** (`%` operator) — Run all early_action functions (tree order)
   214→3. **Main actions** (`/` operator) — Run all action functions (tree order)
   215→4. **Late actions** (`//` operator) — Run all late_action functions (tree order)
   216→5. **Cleanup** — Remove dead objects, remove dead actions, call destroy() hooks
   217→
   218→Objects created during the frame are collected in the next frame (they don't run actions in the frame they're created).
   219→
   220→### Action Phase Execution</code></pre>

</details>

   337→### Action-Based Objects
   338→
   339→Most game objects follow this pattern: create → configure → add behavior → add to tree.
   340→

   343→emoji_particle = class:class_new(object)

<details>
<summary><code>Read (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/CLAUDE.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>318→
   319→**Objects:** Lua tables in a tree. Have state, update, die. When parent dies, children die immediately.
   320→
   321→### Timers, Springs, Colliders
   322→
   323→These are **child objects**, not mixins. They die when their parent dies. No manual cleanup tracking.
   324→
   325→```lua
   326→-- Child object style
   327→self:after(0.5, callback)               -- Timer as action
   328→self + spring('main', 1, 200, 10)       -- Spring as child object
   329→self + collider('tag', 'dynamic', ...)  -- Collider as child object
   330→```
   331→
   332→### Layers</code></pre>

</details>

<details>
<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/CLAUDE.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/CLAUDE.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   317→**Engine (`an`):** C-backed services. Physics, rendering, input, audio, RNG. Always available, don't die.
   318→
   319→**Objects:** Lua tables in a tree. Have state, update, die. When parent dies, children die immediately.
   320→
   321→### Timers, Springs, Colliders
   322→
   323→These are **child objects**, not mixins. They die when their parent dies. No manual cleanup tracking.
   324→
   325→```lua
   326→-- Child object style
   327→self + timer()                          -- Timer as child object
   328→self.timer:after(0.5, callback)         -- Use timer methods
   329→self + spring('main', 1, 200, 10)       -- Spring as child object
   330→self + collider('tag', 'dynamic', ...)  -- Collider as child object
   331→```
   332→
   333→### Layers
   334→
   335→Layers are **engine infrastructure**, created at startup. They're not tree objects. They don't die.</code></pre>

</details>

<details>
<summary><code>Read (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/CLAUDE.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>510→E() ^ {x = 100} &gt;&gt; arena
   511→
   512→-- Named (arena.ball points to this)
   513→E('ball') ^ {x = 100} &gt;&gt; arena
   514→```
   515→
   516→**Timers:**
   517→```lua
   518→-- Anonymous
   519→self:after(0.5, function() self.can_shoot = true end)
   520→
   521→-- Named (self.flash points to this, can be killed/replaced)
   522→self:after(0.15, 'flash', function() self.flashing = false end)
   523→
   524→-- Cancel
   525→if self.flash then self.flash:kill() end
   526→
   527→-- Replace (old 'flash' killed automatically)
   528→self:after(0.15, 'flash', function() self.flashing = false end)
   529→```
   530→
   531→**Actions:**
   532→```lua
   533→-- Anonymous (operators without X)
   534→E() % early_fn / update_fn // late_fn &gt;&gt; arena
   535→
   536→-- Named with X helper (enables cancellation)
   537→self / X('seek', fn)
   538→self % X('water_sim', fn)
   539→self // X('draw', fn)</code></pre>

</details>

   636→end
   637→```
   638→
   639→### Named Timers Pattern
   640→
   641→Use named timers when you need to cancel or replace an ongoing timer:
   642→
   643→```lua
   644→function enemy:flash()
   645→    self.flashing = true
   646→    self:after(0.15, 'flash', function()
   647→        self.flashing = false
   648→    end)
   649→end
   650→
   651→-- Calling flash() again replaces the old timer
   652→-- So rapid hits extend the flash rather than stacking callbacks
   653→
   654→function enemy:stun(duration)
   655→    -- Cancel any existing stun
   656→    if self.stun_timer then self.stun_timer:kill() end
   657→    
   658→    self.stunned = true
   659→    self:after(duration, 'stun_timer', function()
   660→        self.stunned = false
   661→    end)
   662→end
   663→```
   664→</code></pre>

   488→**Multiple `^` operators:** When chaining multiple `^`, group all initialization before action operators:
   489→```lua
   490→-- Good: all init together, then actions
   491→E() ^ {x = 100, y = 200}
   492→   ^ function(self) self:add(timer()) end
   493→   / function(self, dt) ... end
   494→   &gt;&gt; arena
   495→
   496→-- Bad: init split across the chain
   497→E() ^ {x = 100}
   498→   / function(self, dt) ... end

   553→

The main documentation files are now consistent. The `website/context` files will be synced at end of session, and the `yue/*.yue` files are examples from the old LÖVE-based Anchor (not the new engine).

---

## Summary of All Fixes This Session

**Platform & Build:**
- Linux is now a primary platform (not "eventually")
- Build scripts: `build.sh` (Linux), `build-web.sh` (Web), `build.bat` (Windows)

**Colors:**
- Removed `[0]` indexing - now just `an.colors.white`

**Timer Architecture (major clarification):**
- Timer is a child object class, not a mixin
- Usage: `self + timer()` then `self.timer:after(...)` 
- Named callbacks stored on timer: `self.timer.flash`
- Updated all examples in both ANCHOR.md and CLAUDE.md

**Collider/Spring/Timer Model:**
- All are tree objects (classes extending `object`), not factory functions
- Updated "Colliders and Springs Are Factory Functions" → "Colliders, Springs, and Timers Are Tree Objects"

**Lifecycle:**
- Clarified that cleanup phase removes dead objects AND dead actions
- Documented that methods and actions both run (methods first)

**Layer Order:**
- Creation order by default (first = bottom, last = top)
- Can override with `an:set_layer_order({'game', 'effects', 'ui'})`

**Other:**
- Added `rounded_rectangle()` as separate function
- Fixed Drawing example: `self.body` → `self.collider`
- Renamed internal timer array `@timers` → `@callbacks`
- Removed stale `after`/`every` methods from base object class reference

> Please add the following mention (you may rephrase it freely) in every document where timers, springs, colliders are mentioned: these classes are just examples of how the engine handle "engine" objects, but all "engine" classes work like this, they're individual objects instantiated and added as children to their parents. Importantly, they're all (except in exceptional cases) named after themselves. So when you call @ + timer(), timer() is implicitly created as an object named 'timer', which thus means that the parent can access it via @timer. The same applies to colliders, springs, and all other such engine objects.

<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   258→```
   259→
   260→---
   261→
   262→## Colliders, Springs, and Timers
   263→
   264→Everything in Anchor is an object in the tree. Colliders, springs, and timers are objects with specialized functionality — they're added as children just like any other object and die when their parent dies.
   265→
   266→These are examples of **engine objects** — classes that wrap C-side resources (physics bodies, audio handles, etc.). All engine objects follow the same pattern:
   267→
   268→1. They're instantiated and added as children: `@ + timer()`
   269→2. They're named after themselves by default, so `timer()` creates an object with `name = 'timer'`
   270→3. The parent can access them via that name: `@timer:after(0.5, fn)`
   271→
   272→This applies to colliders, springs, timers, and any future engine objects.
   273→
   274→### Collider
   275→
   276→A collider object manages a Box2D physics body. Created via the `collider` factory function:</code></pre>

<details>
<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/CLAUDE.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/CLAUDE.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   317→**Engine (`an`):** C-backed services. Physics, rendering, input, audio, RNG. Always available, don't die.
   318→
   319→**Objects:** Lua tables in a tree. Have state, update, die. When parent dies, children die immediately.
   320→
   321→### Timers, Springs, Colliders
   322→
   323→These are **engine objects** — child objects that wrap C-side resources. They die when their parent dies. No manual cleanup tracking.
   324→
   325→Engine objects are named after themselves by default, so `@ + timer()` creates a child named `'timer'`, accessible via `@timer`. This pattern applies to all engine objects (timers, springs, colliders, and any future ones).
   326→
   327→```lua
   328→-- Engine objects as children
   329→self + timer()                          -- Creates self.timer
   330→self.timer:after(0.5, callback)         -- Access via name
   331→self + spring('main', 1, 200, 10)       -- Named 'main', so self.main
   332→self + collider('player', 'dynamic', 'circle', 10)  -- Named 'collider', so self.collider
   333→```
   334→
   335→### Layers
   336→
   337→Layers are **engine infrastructure**, created at startup. They're not tree objects. They don't die.</code></pre>

   489→- `after(delay, [name], callback)` — one-shot
   490→- `every(interval, [name], callback)` — repeating
   491→- `tween(duration, target, props, easing, [callback])` — interpolation

   494→### 10.4 Spring Object

   496→- `pull(amount)` — displace from target
   497→- `.x` property — current value (updated each frame)
   498→
   499→### 10.5 Collider Object
   500→- Factory: `collider(tag, body_type, shape_type, ...)`
   501→- Wraps Box2D body
   502→- Methods: `get_position`, `set_velocity`, `apply_impulse`, etc.
   503→- Auto-destroys Box2D body on kill
   504→
   505→### 10.6 Update Loop Integration
   506→C calls into Lua each frame:
   507→1. Early phase — all `early_update` and `%` actions
   508→2. Main phase — all `update` and `/` actions
   509→3. Late phase — all `late_update` and `//` actions

<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   482→- Links: `link` for horizontal dependencies
   483→- Operators: `^`, `%`, `/`, `//`, `+`, `&gt;&gt;`
   484→- `__inherited` hook to propagate operators to child classes
   485→
   486→**Ask developer before implementing details.**
   487→
   488→### Engine Object Pattern
   489→
   490→Timer, spring, and collider are examples of **engine objects** — classes that wrap C-side resources. All engine objects follow this pattern:
   491→
   492→1. They extend the base `object` class
   493→2. They're instantiated and added as children: `@ + timer()`
   494→3. They're named after themselves by default (e.g., `timer()` has `name = 'timer'`)
   495→4. Parent accesses them via that name: `@timer:after(...)`
   496→5. They implement `destroy()` to clean up C-side resources when killed
   497→
   498→This pattern applies to any future engine objects (particles, audio sources, etc.).
   499→
   500→### 10.3 Timer Object
   501→- `after(delay, [name], callback)` — one-shot
   502→- `every(interval, [name], callback)` — repeating
   503→- `tween(duration, target, props, easing, [callback])` — interpolation
   504→- Named timers can be cancelled/replaced</code></pre>

</details>

   274→### Collider
   275→
   276→A collider object manages a Box2D physics body:
   277→
   278→```lua
   279→-- @ + collider(tag, body_type, shape_type, ...)
   280→self + collider('player', 'dynamic', 'circle', 12)</code></pre>

</details>

Done. Added the engine object pattern explanation to all three files:

**ANCHOR.md** (Colliders, Springs, and Timers section):
- Explains engine objects wrap C-side resources
- Lists the 3-step pattern: instantiate → named after themselves → access via name
- Notes this applies to all future engine objects

**CLAUDE.md** (Timers, Springs, Colliders section):
- Same explanation in condensed form
- Updated code example with comments showing the naming

**ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md** (Phase 10):
- Added "Engine Object Pattern" subsection before Timer/Spring/Collider
- Lists the 5 implementation requirements
- Mentions future objects (particles, audio sources, etc.)

Also removed "factory function" language from Collider and Spring sections since they're proper classes now.

> Great, any parts of the documents that are still inconsistent, wrong, dubious, or that you have questions about?

Yes, I noticed an inconsistency in the engine object naming:

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

<pre><code>User has answered your questions: "Engine objects are 'named after themselves by default', but spring('main', ...) takes an explicit name as first arg, while collider('player', ...) uses 'player' as physics tag, not tree name. How should this work?"="This is because the "spring" object was actually multiple springs internally, and the user could then access each one individually. This will be different in new Anchor, as if you want to have multiple springs you just create multiple springs and have them as children. By default, each spring is named "spring", but if you want to have multiple then it can also take its name as an argument. So every time you see spring('main') and it's the only spring, that's wrong, unless the code explicitly wants a spring named 'main', which usually isn't the case since self.spring is way more understandable than self.main. But in cases where there are multiple springs, you'd want @ + spring('attack'), @ + spring('hit') and so on.". You can now continue with the user's answers in mind.</code></pre>

</details>

Got it! Springs (and all engine objects) default to their class name, and only take an explicit name when you need multiple. Let me fix the examples:

<details>

docs/ANCHOR.md:301:self + spring('shoot', 1, 300, 15)
docs/ANCHOR.md:360:    self + spring('main', 1, 200, 10)</code></pre>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/CLAUDE.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   323→These are **engine objects** — child objects that wrap C-side resources. They die when their parent dies. No manual cleanup tracking.
   324→
   325→Engine objects are named after themselves by default, so `@ + timer()` creates a child named `'timer'`, accessible via `@timer`. This pattern applies to all engine objects (timers, springs, colliders, and any future ones).
   326→
   327→```lua
   328→-- Engine objects as children (default names)
   329→self + timer()                          -- Creates self.timer
   330→self + spring(1, 200, 10)               -- Creates self.spring
   331→self + collider('player', 'dynamic', 'circle', 10)  -- Creates self.collider
   332→
   333→-- Multiple of same type (explicit names)
   334→self + spring('attack', 1, 200, 10)     -- Creates self.attack
   335→self + spring('hit', 1, 300, 15)        -- Creates self.hit
   336→```
   337→
   338→### Layers
   339→
   340→Layers are **engine infrastructure**, created at startup. They're not tree objects. They don't die.</code></pre>

</details>

<summary><code>Read (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>398→---
   399→
   400→## Drawing
   401→
   402→Objects draw themselves in their update function. Drawing is decoupled from tree structure — an object can draw to any layer.
   403→
   404→```lua
   405→function player:update(dt)
   406→    -- Update logic
   407→    self.x, self.y = self.collider:get_position()
   408→    
   409→    -- Drawing to game layer
   410→    game:push(self.x, self.y, self.r, self.main.x, self.main.x)
   411→        game:circle(self.x, self.y, 10, self.color)
   412→    game:pop()
   413→    
   414→    -- Drawing to effects layer
   415→    if self.flashing then
   416→        effects:circle(self.x, self.y, 15, colors.white)
   417→    end
   418→end
   419→```
   420→
   421→Draw order within a layer is determined by submission order (the order Lua calls draw functions), not tree order.
   422→</code></pre>

</details>

   400→## Drawing

   403→

   405→function player:update(dt)
   406→    -- Update logic

   417→    end

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   496→5. They implement `destroy()` to clean up C-side resources when killed
   497→
   498→This pattern applies to any future engine objects (particles, audio sources, etc.).
   499→
   500→### 10.3 Timer Object
   501→- Constructor: `timer([name])` — name optional, defaults to 'timer'
   502→- `after(delay, [name], callback)` — one-shot
   503→- `every(interval, [name], callback)` — repeating
   504→- `tween(duration, target, props, easing, [callback])` — interpolation
   505→- Named callbacks can be cancelled/replaced
   506→
   507→### 10.4 Spring Object

   752→
   753→## YueScript Alternative
   754→
   755→*YueScript (evolved from MoonScript) is a language that compiles to Lua. It offers significant syntactic improvements: significant whitespace, `@` for self, `=&gt;` for methods with implicit self, no `end` keywords, `+=`/`-=` operators, and optional parentheses. The operator-based object construction style fits YueScript's aesthetic particularly well.*
   756→
   757→### Single-Letter Aliases in YueScript</code></pre>

---

## Session Summary

**Major changes made:**

1. **Linux as primary platform** - Updated from "eventually" to primary alongside Windows and Web

2. **Build scripts** - `build.sh` (Linux), `build-web.sh` (Web), `build.bat` (Windows)

3. **Timer architecture overhaul** - Timer is now a child object, not a mixin:
   - `self + timer()` then `self.timer:after(...)`
   - Callbacks stored on timer: `self.timer.flash`

4. **Engine object pattern documented** - All engine objects (timer, spring, collider):
   - Default to class name: `timer()` → `self.timer`
   - Optional explicit name for multiples: `spring('attack', ...)` → `self.attack`

5. **Removed "factory function" concept** - All are proper tree objects extending `object`

6. **Spring examples fixed** - Single springs now use `spring(1, 200, 10)` with `self.spring`, not `spring('main', ...)`

7. **Collider naming** - Optional name first, then tag: `collider([name], tag, body_type, shape_type, ...)`

8. **Color format simplified** - `an.colors.white` instead of `an.colors.white[0]`

9. **Methods + actions both run** - Documented that methods run first, then actions

10. **Layer order** - Creation order by default, overridable with `an:set_layer_order()`

11. **Cleanup phase** - Handles dead objects AND dead actions

12. **Renamed internal timer array** - `@timers` → `@callbacks` to avoid confusion

<details>
<summary><code>Read (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>1→# Anchor Implementation Plan
     2→
     3→C engine with YueScript scripting, OpenGL rendering, targeting Windows, Linux, and Web.
     4→
     5→---
     6→
     7→## Summary of Key Decisions
     8→
     9→| Area | Decision | Rationale |
    10→|------|----------|-----------|
    11→| Renderer | OpenGL | Smooth rotation, additive blending, performance headroom, console-portable |
    12→| Audio | TBD (miniaudio or SoLoud) | Need pitch shifting; SDL_mixer insufficient |
    13→| Physics | Box2D 3.1 | Already used, true ball-to-ball collisions needed |
    14→| Scripting | Lua 5.4 + YueScript | Build-time compilation with `-r` flag for line numbers |
    15→| Timestep | Fixed 60fps | Determinism matters for potential replay support |
    16→| Resolution | Per-game configurable | 480×270, 640×360, or custom; integer scaling |
    17→| C Structure | Single anchor.c | Monolithic file, easier navigation |
    18→| Resources | Live forever | Games are small enough; no unloading needed |
    19→| Distribution | Single executable | Assets embedded |
    20→
    21→---
    22→
    23→## Build Strategy
    24→
    25→### YueScript Compilation
    26→
    27→**Build-time compilation** — compile `.yue` → `.lua` during build, not at runtime.
    28→
    29→```
    30→game.yue ──► yue -r ──► game.lua ──► embedded in executable

After reading all three documents fresh, here's my consistency analysis:

## Issues Found

### 1. `add` vs `add_child` Method Inconsistency

**Examples throughout both docs use `add()`:**
- ANCHOR.md line 151: `parent:add(child)`
- ANCHOR.md line 523: `self:add(timer())`
- CLAUDE.md line 470: `self:add(timer())`

**But the reference implementation only defines `add_child()`:**
- ANCHOR.md lines 969-977 and 1096-1098 show `add_child` as the method name

The `>>` operator implementation uses `add_child`:
```yuescript
__shr: (other) =>
  other\add_child @
  other
```

**This needs reconciliation.** Either:
- Add `add = add_child` alias to the reference implementation, OR
- Change all examples to use `add_child`

Given that examples consistently use `add()`, I'd suggest adding the alias to the reference implementation.

### 2. Minor: Build Function Example Style

In CLAUDE.md lines 467-475, the build function example uses `self:add(timer())`:
```lua
E() ^ function(self)
          self.x, self.y = 100, 200
          self:add(timer())
      end
```

While this works, the `+` operator is the preferred syntax everywhere else. Could be:
```lua
E() ^ function(self)
          self.x, self.y = 100, 200
          self + timer()
      end
```

---

## Everything Else Looks Consistent

- **Engine object default naming** (`timer()` → `self.timer`, `spring(1,200,10)` → `self.spring`, `collider('tag',...)` → `self.collider`) — consistent across all three docs
- **Collider signature** `collider([name], tag, body_type, shape_type, ...)` — consistent
- **Spring signature** `spring([name], initial, stiffness, damping)` — consistent
- **Timer pattern** `self + timer()` then `self.timer:after(...)` — consistent, no old `self:after()` patterns remain
- **Color format** `an.colors.white` without `[0]` — consistent
- **Platforms** Windows, Linux, Web — consistent
- **Build scripts** `build.sh`, `build.bat`, `build-web.sh` — consistent
- **Layer order** creation order, overridable via `set_layer_order()` — documented
- **Methods + actions both run** — documented in ANCHOR.md
- **Cleanup removes dead actions** — documented in update order
- **`rounded_rectangle()` function** — in both ANCHOR.md and IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md

---

Would you like me to fix the `add`/`add_child` inconsistency by adding an alias to the reference implementation?

> There should be no alias, it should just be the add function, no add_child, any usages of it must be wrong for some reason. As for the minor issue, yes, sure, use the + syntax.

I'll fix both issues - changing `add_child` to `add` in the reference implementation, and updating the build function example to use `+`.

> Alright, given everything you now know about the engine, and given what's in the actual documents, including the context brief about *why* these decisions were made, what's your opinion on Anchor as an engine? Do the stated philosophical principles actually play out in the actual usage of the engine? Does it seem like it's a good engine to make games with? Does it feel elegant? Etc. Give me your full opinion. This is essentially the same consistency check, except at a higher, more soft level of analysis. ultrathink

This is a great question. Let me think through this carefully.

## Does the Philosophy Actually Manifest?

### Locality - Yes, Genuinely Achieved

The operator syntax delivers on its promise. This:

```yuescript
E 'ball' ^ {x: 240, y: 135, vx: 100, vy: 100}
         / (dt) =>
             @x += @vx * dt
             game\circle @x, @y, 8, an.colors.white
         >> arena
```

...genuinely contains everything about the ball in one expression: name, initial state, behavior, tree position. Compare to Unity (separate script file, prefab, inspector values, spawning code) or even plain LÖVE (class definition, update function, draw function, adding to a list). Anchor collapses all of that.

The `>>` operator is particularly well-chosen. "Flow this object into that parent" reads left-to-right naturally. The whole pipeline (`create → configure → add behavior → place in tree`) is visible in reading order.

### No Bureaucracy - Yes, Actually Achieved

The globals being "just there" (`an`, `E`, `X`, `A`) is genuinely liberating. No imports, no registration, no dependency injection ceremony.

The engine object pattern is elegant:
- `self + timer()` and it exists as `self.timer`
- No manual cleanup ever
- No "remember to destroy this in your destructor"

The default naming convention (`timer()` → `self.timer`) is a small touch that eliminates boilerplate without feeling magical. You know what it does, it just saves you typing `self.timer = timer()`.

### Tree-Based Ownership - The Core Insight

This is the most elegant part of the design. The observation that "parent dies → children die" captures a huge percentage of real game relationships:
- Player dies → weapons, effects, UI indicators die
- Enemy dies → health bar, AI state, particles die  
- Arena/room dies → everything in it dies

And the `link()` escape hatch for horizontal dependencies (siblings, cousins) handles the cases where tree structure doesn't fit, with clear guidance on when to use each.

The named child replacement behavior (adding `'ball'` kills the existing `'ball'`) is subtle but powerful — it handles "respawn" patterns without explicit cleanup code.

### Alien But Right - Mostly, With Reservations

The syntax earns most of its complexity. Once internalized, `E 'ball' ^ {...} / fn >> arena` is faster to read and write than verbose alternatives.

However, some operator choices feel arbitrary:

- **`//` for late_action** — In most languages `//` is a comment or floor division. YueScript uses `--` for comments, so there's no conflict, but programmers from other backgrounds will do a double-take. It's also visually noisy.

- **`%` for early_action** — There's no mnemonic. `^` suggests "power up/initialize", `/` suggests "action/do something", but `%` for "before"? It's just... chosen.

- **The `X` helper** — `X('name', fn)` returning `{[name]: fn}` which the operator detects and unpacks is clever but magical. The method alternative (`self:action('name', fn)`) is more verbose but more obvious. The `X` works, but it's a layer of indirection you have to internalize.

These are minor quibbles. The overall syntax does earn its weirdness.

## Tensions and Potential Issues

### 1. Collision Handling Breaks Locality

The documented pattern puts collision logic in arena:

```lua
function arena:update(dt)
    for _, c in ipairs(an:physics_get_contact_enter('player', 'enemy')) do
        local player, enemy = c.a, c.b
        player:take_damage(enemy.damage)
    end
end
```

This is rules-based collision handling. The player's response to hitting an enemy is defined in arena, not in player. 

The documentation acknowledges this ("rules-based code can live inside objects when it's self-contained"), and the rationale is sound (Box2D event queries are cleaner than callback hell). But it does mean collision-related behavior is scattered.

This isn't a flaw — it's a conscious tradeoff. But it's worth noting that locality has limits, and collision handling is where those limits appear.

### 2. Draw Order vs Tree Order

Draw order is submission order, not tree order. This is the right choice (flexibility), but it means the visual layering of objects isn't expressed where the objects are defined.

If you want all enemies behind all players, you either:
- Use different layers
- Carefully order your draw calls
- Accept that tree structure doesn't help here

Again, conscious tradeoff, correctly made. But locality doesn't extend to visual ordering.

### 3. Timer/Spring/Collider Unification

These are all "engine objects" with the same pattern, but they feel conceptually different:

- **Timer** — purely temporal, no spatial component
- **Spring** — animation/interpolation, has a value that changes  
- **Collider** — physics presence, has position/velocity/forces

Grouping them reduces cognitive load ("they all work the same way"), but the abstraction papers over real differences. A timer is a "capability" you add; a collider is a "physical presence." These feel different even if they're implemented similarly.

### 4. YueScript Lock-in

The engine is deeply tied to YueScript: operator overloading, `@` for self, the class system. Plain Lua users can't use the nice syntax at all.

This is fine — the developer chose YueScript deliberately for its aesthetics. But it means:
- Any YueScript bugs become engine limitations
- The `-r` flag is critical (without it, errors point to wrong lines)
- Development is YueScript-or-bust

Build-time compilation mitigates some risk (no runtime YueScript dependency), but the ergonomics assume YueScript.

### 5. The Reference Implementation's Status

The YueScript reference implementation in ANCHOR.md shows how the object system works internally. But it's unclear if this is:
- Prescriptive (implement exactly this)
- Illustrative (shows the concept, C implementation may differ)

For a C engine with Lua scripting, the actual object system will be partially in C. The reference implementation is useful for understanding, but the relationship to final implementation is fuzzy.

## What's Genuinely Elegant

1. **The tree model** — Simple, powerful, eliminates orphaned objects and manual cleanup entirely

2. **Engine objects as children** — Timer, spring, collider becoming tree nodes with automatic lifecycle is clever and consistent

3. **The operator pipeline** — `E() ^ {...} / fn >> parent` is genuinely expressive

4. **Default naming** — `timer()` → `self.timer` without explicit assignment

5. **Named child replacement** — Adding a child with an existing name kills the old one automatically

6. **Methods + actions both run** — Class-defined behavior coexists with dynamically added behavior cleanly

## What Could Be Better

1. **Operator choices** — `//` and `%` feel arbitrary; better mnemonics would help

2. **Collision handling location** — Necessary tradeoff but breaks locality

3. **The `X` helper** — Works but feels like a magic escape hatch

## Is It Good for Making Games?

**Yes, for its intended use case.**

Anchor is designed for:
- Solo developers
- Action games (physics-heavy, juicy, lots of objects)
- Small-to-medium scope
- Developers who value code aesthetics

For this profile, it looks excellent. The locality principle serves solo workflow. The tree model handles 90% of object lifetime. The terse syntax reduces friction.

It would NOT be good for:
- Large teams (globals and implicit behavior cause coordination problems)
- Non-YueScript users (syntax advantages disappear)
- Games with complex many-to-many relationships
- Developers who prefer explicit over implicit

## Does It Feel Elegant?

**Mostly yes.**

The elegance comes from consistent application of a coherent vision. The tree model, the operator syntax, the engine object pattern — they all reinforce each other. The stated principles (locality, no bureaucracy, tree ownership) are actually reflected in the API, not just marketing.

The test of elegance: does the complexity serve a purpose? For most of Anchor's unusual choices, yes. The weirdness buys terseness and expressiveness. A few choices (`//`, `%`, `X`) feel like they could be refined, but they're not deal-breakers.

## The Deeper Question

Reading the Context Brief, I notice the developer works "primarily by intuition — choices are made because they 'feel right' before rational justification."

This shows in the design. Anchor *feels* like a coherent artifact, not a committee-designed API. The operator syntax, the single-letter aliases, the tree model — they have a personality. Someone with strong aesthetic opinions made these choices.

The risk with intuition-driven design is inconsistency or blind spots. But having read all three documents, I don't see that here. The design is consistent. The tradeoffs are acknowledged. The escape hatches exist where they need to.

The real test is whether it feels right when actually making a game. Theory only goes so far. But the design is sound enough that I'd expect the feel to follow — especially since the developer has shipped games before and knows what friction feels like in practice.

**Final assessment:** Anchor is a well-designed engine that genuinely embodies its stated philosophy. The tree model is the core insight, and everything else supports it coherently. The syntax is unusual but earns its complexity. For a solo developer making action games who values code aesthetics, this looks like a genuinely good tool.

> Let's handle each issue individually, what should // and % be changed to? Remember that we are bound to Lua/YueScript operators, and that their order matters since operators have specific ways in which they apply (without having to apply parenthesis).

Good question. Let me look at what's actually available.

**Lua/YueScript operators at the same precedence level as `/`:**
- `*` (multiply)
- `/` (divide) — currently main action
- `//` (floor divide) — currently late_action
- `%` (modulo) — currently early_action

These all chain left-to-right naturally, which is why the current setup works without parentheses.

**The problem:**
- `%` has no mnemonic for "early"
- `//` has no mnemonic for "late" (and looks like comments in other languages)

**A better assignment:**

| Operator | Phase | Mnemonic |
|----------|-------|----------|
| `*` | early_action | Asterisk/star — prominent, foundational, "prepare" |
| `/` | action | Slash — the main action (unchanged) |
| `%` | late_action | Modulo/remainder — "what's left to do after" |

The `%` → late connection is actually decent: modulo gives you the *remainder* after division. Late actions are what *remain* to be done after main processing (often drawing, which needs final positions).

The `*` → early connection is weaker but workable: asterisks mark importance, suggest "everything" (wildcard), visually more prominent than `/`.

The visual progression `*` → `/` → `%` also has coherence — they're all multiplicative-family operators.

```yuescript
E() ^ {x: 100}
   * (dt) => -- early: physics prep
   / (dt) => -- main: logic
   % (dt) => -- late: drawing
   >> arena
```

This frees `//` entirely — we just don't use it. One less operator to learn.

**Alternative: keep `//` as an alias for `%`?** Both have the same precedence, both could mean "late." But that's probably more confusing than helpful — better to have one canonical way.

What do you think of `*` / `/` / `%` for early / main / late?

> Is there a way we could have all actions use / but with some kind of modifier for each?

Interesting idea. Since `/` already detects the `X('name', fn)` pattern (a table with a single string key mapping to a function), we could extend that detection for phase markers.

**Option 1: Full-word helpers**
```yuescript
/ before (dt) => ...    -- early
/ (dt) => ...           -- main
/ after (dt) => ...     -- late
```

Where `before` and `after` are functions that wrap the callback in a detectable table. Clear, but longer.

**Option 2: Single-letter helpers**
```yuescript
/ B (dt) => ...    -- Before (early)
/ (dt) => ...      -- main
/ L (dt) => ...    -- Late
```

`B` breaks the runic alphabet rule (it's round), but the mnemonic is strong. `L` is in the runic set and works.

**Option 3: Runic-only (weaker mnemonics)**
```yuescript
/ U (dt) => ...    -- Under/underlying (early, foundational)
/ (dt) => ...      -- main
/ L (dt) => ...    -- Late
```

`U` is runic and "underlying" sort of works for "foundational stuff that runs first."

**Option 4: Prefix convention with X**
```yuescript
/ X '<', (dt) => ...    -- < means before (early)
/ (dt) => ...           -- main
/ X '>', (dt) => ...    -- > means after (late)
```

Reuses X, uses visual arrows. Named versions: `X '<physics', fn`, `X '>draw', fn`.

---

For named actions in options 1-3, we'd need combined helpers or a signature extension:
```yuescript
/ before 'physics', (dt) => ...   -- named early
/ X 'update', (dt) => ...         -- named main
/ after 'draw', (dt) => ...       -- named late
```

Which of these directions feels right to you? Or should I explore something else?

> Can you show me a few complete code examples using U, L and X '<' X '>'? So I can see what it looks like. Actually, create a .yue file in the yue/ folder so I can open it on my editor.

<details>
<summary><code>Write (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/yue/operator_comparison.yue)</code></summary>

<pre><code>File created successfully at: /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/yue/operator_comparison.yue</code></pre>

</details>

Created `/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/yue/operator_comparison.yue`. 

Key observations from writing these out:

**U/L approach:**
- Anonymous early/late are very clean: `/ U (dt) =>`, `/ L (dt) =>`
- Named versions need separate helpers: `/ U 'physics', (dt) =>`, `/ L 'draw', (dt) =>`
- Introduces two new single-letter globals (U and L)

**X prefix approach:**
- Anonymous early/late require X: `/ X '<', (dt) =>` — more verbose
- Named versions are compact: `/ X '<physics', (dt) =>` — the prefix is part of the name
- Reuses existing X helper, no new globals
- The `<` and `>` have natural "before/after" meaning (arrows pointing left/right on a timeline)

The tradeoff is basically:
- **U/L**: Cleaner for anonymous actions, but adds two globals
- **X prefix**: More verbose for anonymous, but unified under one mechanism

> We could use E for early and another letter for object itself. What do you think?

Interesting idea. If E becomes Early, we need a new letter for object. From the runic set (E, X, A, T, L, V, U, Y, I, H):

| Letter | Mnemonic | Feel |
|--------|----------|------|
| **O** | Object | Obvious, but breaks runic rule (round) |
| **T** | Thing | Casual, clear, runic |
| **U** | Unit | Game-y, runic |
| **I** | Instance | Technical, runic |

How they'd read:

```yuescript
-- O for Object (breaks runic, but immediately obvious)
O 'ball' ^ {x: 100}
   / E (dt) => -- early
   / (dt) => -- main
   / L (dt) => -- late
   >> arena

-- T for Thing (runic, casual)
T 'ball' ^ {x: 100}
   / E (dt) =>
   / (dt) =>
   / L (dt) =>
   >> arena
```

**My take:**

`O` for Object is the most intuitive — zero learning curve. Breaking the runic rule might be worth it for the most frequently used alias.

`T` for Thing is the best runic option. "Thing" is how we casually refer to game objects anyway ("spawn a thing", "kill that thing"). It's unpretentious.

`U` and `I` feel like stretches.

The E/L pairing for Early/Late is strong — clear mnemonics, both runic. The question is whether `O` or `T` feels right for object creation.

Want me to update the comparison file with O and T variants so you can see them in your editor?

> Let's try A. Recreate the file with all examples, E for early, A for object.

Done. The file now uses:
- **A** = object constructor
- **E** = early action  
- **L** = late action
- **X** = named (main) action

Note: A was previously the alias for `all()`. The file shows using the full `@\all 'tag'` method, or we could use **Q** for Query if you want to keep a single-letter alias for that.

The mnemonic for A as object is loose — could be "A thing" (indefinite article) or "Add to tree". Not as strong as E for Entity was, but E for Early is a better mnemonic fit.

> Let's try Y instead of A.

   141→           @x += @v * math.cos(@r) * dt
   142→           @y += @v * math.sin(@r) * dt
   143→           @duration -= dt
   144→           return true if @duration &lt;= 0
   145→           effects\circle @x, @y, 3, colors.white

   189→-- E = early action (mnemonic: Early)
   190→-- N = named action (mnemonic: Named) -- breaks runic rule but clear mnemonic
   191→-- L = late action (mnemonic: Late)
   192→-- A = all() query (mnemonic: All)
   193→</code></pre>

</details>

Done. Now using:
- **X** = object constructor
- **E** = early action (Early)
- **N** = named action (Named) — breaks runic but strongest mnemonic
- **L** = late action (Late)
- **A** = all() query (All)

X for object has no obvious mnemonic, but it's visually striking and the most "runic" looking letter. N for Named is clear even though it breaks the angular rule.

Alternative for N if you want to stay runic: **T** for "Tagged" — the name acts like a tag for the action.

> Nah, let's settle on E for object, U for early, L for late, X for named, A for all.

<pre><code>1→# Anchor Engine Specification
     2→
     3→*Anchor is a 2D game engine built around locality of behavior and minimal cognitive overhead. Code that belongs together stays together.*

    30→### Locality of Behavior
